class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D447%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DNana%2BWang%252C%2BZhongchao%2BBai%252C%2BYitai%2BQian%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b13193%26title%3DOne-Dimensional%2BYolk%25E2%2580%2593Shell%2BSb%2540Ti%25E2%2580%2593O%25E2%2580%2593P%2BNanostructures%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Capacity%2Band%2BHigh-Rate%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D454%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2017" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Development of high energy/power density and long cycle life of anode materials is highly desirable for sodium ion batteries, because graphite anode cannot be used directly. Sb stands out from the potential candidates, due to high capacity, good electronic conductivity, and moderate sodiation voltage. Here, one-dimensional yolk–shell Sb@Ti–O–P nanostructures are synthesized by reducing core–shell Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods with NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub>. This structure has Sb nanorod as the core to increase the capacity and Ti–O–P as the shell to stabilize the interface between electrolyte and electrode material. The gap between the core and the shell accommodates the volume change during sodiation/desodiation. These features endow the structure outstanding performances. It could deliver a capacity of about 760 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, with a capacity retention of about 94%. Even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity is still at 360 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The full battery of Sb@Ti–O–P//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>–C presents a high output voltage (∼2.7 V) and a capacity of 392 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> after 150 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D447%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DNana%2BWang%252C%2BZhongchao%2BBai%252C%2BYitai%2BQian%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D9%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b13193%26title%3DOne-Dimensional%2BYolk%25E2%2580%2593Shell%2BSb%2540Ti%25E2%2580%2593O%25E2%2580%2593P%2BNanostructures%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Capacity%2Band%2BHigh-Rate%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D454%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291129&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanorods" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanorods" class="article__tags__link">Nanorods</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291675&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stability" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Stability" class="article__tags__link">Stability</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Antimony&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_antimony">antimony</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Composite&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_composite">composite</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanostructures&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanostructures">nanostructures</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Full+Cells&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_full%20cells">full cells</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Lithium ion batteries (LIBs) have been successfully commercialized in the past decades and extensively integrated into portable electronics, automobiles, and hybrid electric vehicles. However, the unevenly distributed resources and low abundance of lithium on earth could not satisfy the growing consumption. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Recently, sodium ion batteries (NIBs) have gained significant attention as an ideal alternative to LIBs to address these concerns. But the commercialized anode for LIBs, graphite, does not work for NIBs, because its interlayer distance is not large enough to hold Na ions due to their large cation radii (1.02 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> The similar case also happens to silicon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Thus, many efforts have been devoted to seek alternative materials. As a result, a number of candidates start to come into sight, including metal sulfides, metal oxides, organic polymers, and metallic alloys. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 1 and 5 to 7">(1, 5-7)</a> In these candidates, Sb stands out from the rest, due to its high theoretical capacity (∼660 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), good electronic conductivity, and moderate sodiation voltage. But the big volume change during sodiation/desodiation processes, which could cause structure fracture and lead to inferior electronic conductivity and poor cycling stability, degrades its performances as the anode material. This case is also encountered in the alloy-type anode materials of LIBs. So, the solutions to this issue could be borrowed for NIBs, including size/shape control (nanoparticles or nanorods), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8, 9)</a> structure engineering (nanotubes, nanorod arrays, or hollow nanospheres), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 10 to 14">(10-14)</a> surface modification (carbon, TiO<sub>2</sub>, or Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 12 to 15">(12-15)</a> and component modulation (Sb/graphene, Sb/MWCNT, Cu<sub>2</sub>Sb, or ZnSb). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 16 to 20">(16-20)</a> These strategies have remarkably enhanced the performances of Sb. In spite of this, there are still many challenges in front of the application. For instance, the porous structure, which is well-known for its enhancement on cycling stability and rate capability, also reduces the packing density and decreases the initial Coulombic efficiency due to the large specific surface area. Recently reported Sb/TiO<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub> nanotubes and Sb/C nanotubes are typical examples in this regard. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12, 13)</a> Although they exhibit outstanding electrochemical performances, the initial Coulombic efficiency was only ∼65% for Sb/TiO<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub> and 50.6% for Sb/C. Moreover, the empty interior is more than enough to fit the volume change, resulting in the waste of space. Meanwhile, the materials used to stabilize the interface between electrolyte and electrode materials, which are carbon or TiO<sub>2</sub> in most cases, have very limited capacity and greatly lower the composite capacity. Thus, how to design the hollow structure with a high space efficiency, and achieve the stable surface without the cost of composite capacity, is a great challenge to us.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Here, one-dimensional yolk–shell Sb@Ti–O–P nanostructures are synthesized by the reduction of core–shell Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods with NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> at a low temperature. As accompanied by the reduction of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and TiO<sub>2</sub>, phosphorus is anchored in the shell via the formation of P–O bonds, generating the unique Ti–O–P shell. This yolk–shell structure well-addresses the above concerns, because the empty interior is occupied by high-capacity Sb nanorods. Meanwhile, the Ti–O–P shell offers a robust and stable surface between electrolyte and electrode material. The gap between the Sb nanorod and the Ti–O–P shell benefits the releasing of the strain/stress induced by the volume change and improves the space efficiency. What’s more, the surface oxidation of the Sb nanorods increases the composite capacity. To our knowledge, such a Sb-based structure has not been reported before. As expected, this structure delivers improved specific capacity, and outstanding rate capability. It could stay at 760 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity is still kept at 360 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The initial Coulombic efficiency could be promoted to 75.0%. After being matched with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C for a full cell, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> this structure exhibits a high output voltage and a high capacity.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis of yolk–shell Sb@Ti–O–P nanostructures is briefly presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. First, high-quality Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanorods with their lengths up to hundreds of micrometers were obtained by a simple and efficient process at room temperature (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a> Figure S1). Then, an amorphous TiO<sub>2</sub> layer was coated on the surface of these nanorods via a controlled hydrolysis of TBOT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). As the core–shell Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods were heated with NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> together, NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> underwent disproportionation, releasing gaseous and reductive PH<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> was reduced to metallic Sb that gradually shrank and detached from the TiO<sub>2</sub> shell. Such a yolk–shell structure facilitates the anode material to keep its stability during repeated sodiation/desodiation processes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23, 24)</a> Meanwhile, TiO<sub>2</sub> was partially reduced, introducing Ti<sup>3+</sup> and oxygen vacancies into the shell. The formation of oxygen-deficient TiO<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub> could effectively improve the electron conductivity and the charge-transfer kinetics, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 13, 25 and 26">(13, 25, 26)</a> promoting the electrochemical performances. The oxidation product of PH<sub>3</sub> is likely to be fixed on the surface or the subsurface via the formation of P–O. Thus, the final product could be denoted as yolk–shell Sb@Ti–O–P.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0007.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Formation Process of One-Dimensional Yolk–shell Sb@Ti–O–P Nanostructures</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the XRD pattern of Sb@Ti–O–P. All of the diffraction peaks can be indexed as rhombohedral-phase Sb (JCPDS Card No. 35-0732). No diffraction peaks belonging to Ti/PO<sub><i>x</i></sub> are observed in the pattern, indicating their poor crystallinity. In spite of this, EDS spectra clearly confirm the existence of Ti and P in the product (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). This result is also supported by the XPS spectrum of Sb@Ti–O–P, in which the signals of Sb 3d, Ti 2p, and P 2p are easily observed (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). The high-resolution spectra of these signals were measured to gain insights about their valence status. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, the high-resolution spectrum of Sb 3d consists of two doublets, but they have to be deconvoluted into five peaks in view of the overlapping of Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> in Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> with O 1s. The peaks at 527.9 and 537.3 eV could be ascribed to Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> from metallic Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> The peaks at 531.0 and 540.4 eV could be attributed to Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> from SbO<sub><i>x</i></sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Because the peak intensities of SbO<sub><i>x</i></sub> are much higher than those from metallic Sb, it indicates that there is severe surface oxidation on the product. In order to confirm this point, Ar ion sputtering was conducted to remove the surface layer. As presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, the peak intensities of metallic Sb greatly increase, showing a higher ratio of Sb<sup>0</sup>/Sb<sup>3+</sup> relative to the data before the sputtering. This result suggests that metallic Sb basically locates at the core, and its surface is oxidized, which might be caused by the storage in air. The case of Ti 2p is different from that of Sb 3d. There is no metallic Ti in the product, although the fitting of Ti 2p also shows two doublets (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e). The doublet at 459.9 and 465.6 eV is believed to come from Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Ti<sup>4+</sup>–O–P. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28-30)</a> The other at 458.8 and 464.5 eV is consistent with Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Ti<sup>4+</sup>–O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28-30)</a> After the removal of the surface layer, the doublet of the Ti<sup>3+</sup> species at 457.5 and 462.9 eV appears in the product. This result indicates the similar surface oxidation for Ti species. Different from the cases of Ti 2p and Sb 3d, there is no apparent change for P 2p before and after the Ar ion sputtering (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f). The two peaks at 133.9 and 134.4 eV are in line with the data of P–O bonds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28-30)</a> It is noted that the ratios of P/Ti and Ti/Sb after Ar ion sputtering greatly decrease, consistent with their unique structure as illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD pattern, (b) EDX spectra, (c) survey spectra, and (d–f) high-resolution spectra of Sb 3d, Ti 2p, and P 2p in Sb@Ti–O–P.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows a typical SEM image of Sb@Ti–O–P, where the one-dimensional structure of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is well-retained after the controlled hydrolysis for the TiO<sub>2</sub> shell and the low-temperature heating for Sb and Ti–O–P. The high-magnification SEM image on the tip of these structures (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) shows a nanorod at the core detached from the external shell, as highlighted by the red circles. Such a yolk–shell structure is in a high yield in the product, because this feature could be visualized on most of the particles. This result is also supported by TEM images. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c, there is a significant gap between the shell and the core, well-consistent with the yolk–shell structure. The careful observation further discloses that the nanorod contains multiple pores with their sizes in tens of nanometers, which agrees well with the pore size distribution obtained from nitrogen sorption isotherms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The formation of the pores likely comes from the escape of gaseous H<sub>2</sub>O produced by the reduction of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. The HRTEM image on the shell presents distorted and dim lattice fringes from anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d), confirming its poor crystallinity again. The HRTEM image on the core nanorod shows clear lattice fringes with the spacing about 0.31 nm (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e), corresponding to (012) planes of metallic Sb. However, we cannot observe the lattice fringes from any potential compounds that contain phosphorus, no matter at the core or in the shell. Thus, element mapping and line scanning were conducted to disclose its distribution in the yolk–shell structure. As illustrated in panels g and f of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>, metallic Sb basically locates at the core and Ti, O, and P distribute on the shell, forming a yolk–shell Sb@Ti–O–P structure.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a, b) SEM images, (c) TEM image, (d, e) HRTEM images, (f) elemental mapping images, and (g) line scanning profiles of Sb@Ti–O–P.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a> presents the influences of electrolytes (EC/DEC or PC) and binders ((CMC)Na or (PA)Na) on the electrochemical properties, prior to the detailed electrochemical measurements. It is found that Sb@Ti–O–P exhibits the best performances with (PA)Na as the binder and PC as the electrolyte, which could be attributed to the formation of a uniform and dense layer on active materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31, 32)</a> The first discharge/charge capacity is 1092.5/819.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, resulting in an initial Coulombic efficiency of about 75.0%. The capacity loss may be attributed to the decomposition of electrolyte, the formation of a polymeric gel-like film, and irreversible sodium storage either on the surface or inside the nanopores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33-35)</a> The specific capacity after 80 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> is ∼750 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and is much higher than the theoretical data calculated by the weight percentage of Sb and TiO<sub>2</sub> (564.85 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> = 660 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> × 80 wt % + 335 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> × 11 wt %). Even if the contributions of carbon and binder are taken into account (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>), they still do not fill the gap between theoretical capacity and experimental data. So, it is concluded that other components must participate in the electrochemical reactions. Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> on the surface of Sb attracts our attention, because it exhibits a theoretical capacity as high as 1103 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, the conversion reaction of Sb/Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> at ∼1.2 V in the anodic sweep and ∼1.6 V in the cathodic sweep, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35, 36)</a> is in good agreement with what is observed in CV curves of Sb@Ti–O–P (the inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). Because these peaks are broad and weak, they are easy to be neglected or covered by other peaks. To directly confirm the reversible reaction between Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Sb, Sb/Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles were prepared (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>) and tested between 0.02 and 2.5 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>). After the first discharge, the products charged to 1.5 and 2.5 V were taken for HRTEM images. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c</a>, the product charged to 1.5 V is composed by metallic Sb and Na<sub>2</sub>O. But that charged to 2.5 V is identified as Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5d</a>). These results indicate that Sb could be further oxidized to Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> at a high voltage, thus improving the capacity. In view of the excellent performance of Sb@Ti–O–P in (PA)Na/PC, the following tests are conducted with (PA)Na as the binder and PC as the electrolyte.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Effects of binders or electrolytes on the cycling performance of Sb@Ti–O–P. The current density was 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Cyclic voltammograms of Sb@Ti–O–P and (b) cycling performances, and (c) rate performances of Sb@Ti–O–P, Sb, TiO<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>, and P. The cycling test was conducted at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Comparison of the rate performances between Sb@Ti–O–P and the reported works.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The CV curves of Sb@Ti–O–P for the first six cycles are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. The first cathodic sweep shows two peaks, a broad and weak peak at 1.10 V and an intense peak at 0.37 V. The former could be associated with the decomposition of the electrolyte, the formation of a solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer, and the reduction of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> on the surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35, 36)</a> The latter originates from the sodiation processes of Sb. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref37" aria-label="reference 8 and 37">(8, 37)</a> The intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into TiO<sub>2</sub> occurs over a wide range from 1.5 to 0.5 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), which might be covered by the above reactions. In the first anodic sweep, the broad peak could be fitted by three peaks (0.86, 1.08, and 1.40 V), which likely comes from the desodiation processes of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 8 and 35 to 37">(8, 35-37)</a> and the further oxidation of Sb to SbO<sub><i>x</i></sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35, 36)</a> Because of the close voltages, they overlap with each other, resulting in a tailing peak. In the second sweep, the cathodic peak at 1.30 V and the anodic peak tailing above 1.5 V could be still observed, indicating the conversion reaction between Sb and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. From then on, CV curves overlap very well, indicating the good reversibility. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows the cycling performances of Sb@Ti–O–P, Sb nanoparticles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), TiO<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), and commercial P powders (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) at the same current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. In these materials, P powders show the highest sodiation capacity for the first cycle, ∼2000 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. But its first desodiation capacity drops to ∼600 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting a very low Coulombic efficiency. The capacity quickly falls to ∼70 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles, indicating the poor cycling stability. Compared to the case of P powders, Sb nanoparticles are much better in terms of cycling stability, although they also show the obvious degradation after 200 cycles. TiO<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub> exhibits excellent stability throughout the cycling, but its capacity is very limited, ∼140 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Besides these materials, Sb/TiO<sub>2</sub> microtubes obtained by the calcination of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> at 500 °C in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>) are also tested, because they combine the high capacity of Sb with high-stability TiO<sub>2</sub> in one structure. As expected, they show a decent capacity (∼300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and an enhanced stability, but these terms are still worse than those of Sb@Ti–O–P. Sb@Ti–O–P exhibits superior performances in terms of both reversible capacity and cycling stability, much better than the individual component alone. Sb@Ti–O–P could maintain a capacity of 760 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles with a capacity retention of about 94%. The cycled electrode basically keeps the one-dimensional structure of Sb@Ti–O–P, confirming the structure stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Similar conclusions could be also achieved from the rate performances. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c, Sb@Ti–O–P displays excellent reversibility at high rates. Its specific capacity could be preserved at 805, 730, 680, 605, 510, 420, and 360 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the current density of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, much higher than the individual components alone. Such a rate performance is much better than those of Sb-based anodes in the previous works, as listed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 11 to 16 and 38 to 46">(11-16, 38-46)</a> These works include the well-designed Sb nanostructures such as hollow Sb microspheres (313 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> nanoporous Sb powders (420 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> and so on; the diverse Sb/C composites such as Sb/reduced graphene oxides (192 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Sb/C nanofibers (337 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> and Sb/C nanosheets (142 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> etc.; and MSb alloys such as hollow SbNi nanospheres (300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> Zn<sub>4</sub>Sb<sub>3</sub> nanowires (187 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2.07 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and so on. Moreover, the performance of our composite is also superior to many P-based anodes, such as amorphous red phosphorus confined in a mesoporous carbon matrix (CMK-3; 227 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 8.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 9.8 C), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> red phosphorus entangled with single-walled carbon nanotubes (300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> and so on.</div><div class="NLM_p">The charge/discharge curves of Sb@Ti–O–P at different rates are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. The discharge plateau is at ∼0.7–0.3 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>, which could prevent the formation of sodium dendrites and alleviate the safety risks. At the same time, this plateau also ensures the full cell based on this anode has a high energy density. The cycling performance at a high current density was also evaluated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. Sb@Ti–O–P demonstrates an initial Coulombic efficiency of about 72% and maintains above 99% in the subsequent cycles. After being cycled at 3 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 300 times, it still preserves a capacity of 346 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of 88%. The excellent electrochemical properties of Sb@Ti–O–P could be associated with the effective cooperation of multiple components in a favorable structure. Metallic Sb is present as the core to enhance the charge-transfer kinetics. Meanwhile, Sb has a fair theoretical capacity (∼660 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). Although these data are not comparable to that of phosphorus, they are higher than many anode materials (TiO, 330 mA h g; carbon, ∼300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Ti–O–P is present here as the shell to construct a stable surface with the electrolyte. Meanwhile, the self-doping of Ti<sup>3+</sup> in TiO<sub>2</sub> promotes the electron conductivity. On the other hand, the yolk–shell structure assembled by these components as Sb@Ti–O–P effectively tolerates the volume change upon cycling and makes the structure stable. This result is briefly depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. All these features, not only from the component itself but also from their combination mode, make the composite excellent in sodium storage in terms of cycling stability and rate capability.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Discharge/charge curves of Sb@Ti–O–P at different rates, (b) cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency of Sb@Ti–O–P at 3 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and (c) schematic illustration on the sodiation/desodiation process of Sb@Ti–O–P.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Inspired by the excellent performance of Sb@Ti–O–P in half-cells, the full cells with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C as a cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>) have been assembled and tested. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a presents the cycling performance of the full cell within 1.2–3.8 V at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. After 150 cycles, the cell still preserves a discharge capacity of 392 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> and a high Coulombic efficiency (&gt;98%). The capacity loss in the first several cycles can be ascribed to the SEI film formation and partly irreversible utilization of Na<sup>+</sup> by Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b displays the charge/discharge curves of the full cell at different current densities. The cell could display the capacity of 737, 627, 536, 483, 423, and 382 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. As the current densities increase from 0.1 to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, the output voltage decreases from 2.72 to 2.65 V, due to the polarization of the anode component and cycling at large current densities. Compared to the previous reports about Na-ion full batteries (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//MoO, 1.4 V and 164 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> after 14 cycles; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//FeSe, 1.7 V and 298 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> after 150 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> and Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, 2.8 V and 85 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> after 150 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> the output voltage and cycling capacity have been improved a lot by Sb@Ti–O–P//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C, indicating the promising potential for it to be used in sodium ion batteries.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/medium/am-2016-13193y_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency and (b) charge/discharge curves of Sb@Ti–O–P//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>–C full battery.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/asset/images/large/am-2016-13193y_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b13193&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the one-dimensional yolk–shell nanostructure of Sb@Ti–O–P, has been successfully synthesized via a simple process. Because this structure is not airtight, the surface of the Sb nanorod at the core is oxidized, due to the exposure to air. Similar surface oxidation also happens to Ti and P at the shell, reducing the content of Ti<sup>3+</sup> species and resulting in the formation of P–O. In spite of this, Sb@Ti–O–P still shows excellent electrochemical performances. It presents a capacity of 760 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles at a current density of 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of about 94% relative to the first charge capacity. Even at 3 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 300 cycles, the capacity levels off at 346 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. Its high rate capability is also confirmed by 360 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. All these results are much better than the individual components alone. Sb@Ti–O–P, paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C as a full cell, could reach a capacity of 392 mA h g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub> after 150 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, disclosing its promising potential in the practical applications. These superior performances could be attributed to the good electronic conductivity of Sb, stable cycling stability of TiO<sub>2</sub> in Ti–O–P, and the participation of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> in sodiation/desodiation. Meanwhile, the unique yolk–shell structure benefits the releasing of interior strain and stress and keeps the structure stable. It is believed that this method offers a new pathway to fabricate high-capacity and long-lived anode materials for sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">Antimony (Sb) powders (18 μm, 120 mg) were stirred in an aqueous solution containing poly(vinylpyrrolidone) (PVP; MW 58000, 400 mg) and ethylenediamine (EDA; 9 mmol) at 25 °C for 16 h. The white precipitate was filtrated and dried in vacuum at 60 °C to get Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanorods. Then, Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanorods (120 mg) and ammonia (0.2 mL, 28 wt %) were added into ethanol (40 mL). After the dispersion was treated by ultrasound for 30 min, tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT; 0.4 mL) was added dropwise. The solution was stirred at 45 °C for 10 h. The resultant product, Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods, was filtrated and washed with ethanol for three times.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Synthesis of Sb@Ti–O–P</h3><div class="NLM_p last">NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> were placed into two ceramic boats. The boat with Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> was at the downstream inside the furnace. The weight ratio of NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>2</sub> to Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> was controlled at 6:0.1. Then, they were kept at 280 °C in Ar for 2 h. After being cooled to room temperature, the black product was collected for structure characterization and electrochemical measurements.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure of the products was characterized by X-ray diffractometer equipped with a graphite monochromator (Cu Kα radiation, λ = 1.5418 Å, Bruker D8 Adv, Karlsruhe, Germany). Size and morphology of the products were pictured by field-emission scanning electron microscope (SEM; SUPRA 55, Carl Zeiss, Oberkochen, Germany), transmission electron microscope (TEM; JEOL JEM 1011, Tokyo, Japan), and high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM; JEOL 2100). Thermal gravimetric (TG) analysis was performed on a thermal analyzer (Mettler Toledo TGA/SDTA851, Greifensee, Switzerland). X-ray photoelectron spectra (XPS) were acquired from X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (ESCALAB 250, Thermo Fischer Scientific, Waltham, MA, USA) to identify the chemical components and their valences.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrode was made from active material (70 wt %), conductive carbon black (20 wt %), and sodium polyacrylate ((PA)Na; 10 wt %) or sodium carboxyl methyl cellulose ((CMC)Na; 10 wt %) dispersed in droplets of distilled water. The as-formed slurry was sonicated at room temperature for a half-hour. Then, it was spread over a copper foil cleaned by ethanol for several times. After being dried in vacuum at 60 °C for 12 h, the copper foil was punched into discs with a mass loading of ∼1 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The working electrode was assembled with metallic sodium as the anode and glass microfibers of Whatman GF/F as the separator into coin cells (2032) in a glovebox full of argon (Mikrouna, Super 1220/750/900). The electrolyte was either 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) or 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1:1 (v/v)) containing 3.5% (vol.) of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Glavanostatic discharge/charge cycling was conducted on battery cyclers (Land-CT2001A, Land Electronics Co., Wuhan, China). Here, the current density and the reversible capacity are reported based on the total weight of Sb@Ti–O–P composite, unless specifically notified otherwise. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was carried out on electrochemical workstations (LK 2005A, Lanlike Chemical and Electronic High-Tech. Co., Tianjin, China) with a scanning rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. For the Na ion full cell, homemade Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> acetylene black, and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) in a weight ratio of 75:15:10 were used to fabricate the cathode using Al foil as the current collector. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Before the use in the full cell, the anode was electrochemically activated for three cycles to eliminate the huge capacity loss in the first cycle. For the full cells, the voltage cutoff window was 1.0–3.8 V, and the excess capacity of cathode was controlled at ∼10% while the anode was limited.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b13193" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b13193</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern and SEM images of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods, nitrogen sorption isotherm of Sb@Ti–O–P; cycling performances of carbon, binder, and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>; XRD pattern and discharge/charge profiles of Sb/Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles; HRTEM images of Sb/Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles charged to 1.5 and 2.5 V after the first discharge; CV curves of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>; XRD pattern and TEM image of Sb nanocrystals; TEM images of TiO<sub>2</sub> and P particles; cycling performance of P particles; SEM and TEM images of cycled Sb@Ti–O–P; and XRD pattern, TEM image, charge/discharge curves, and cycling performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b13193/suppl_file/am6b13193_si_001.pdf">am6b13193_si_001.pdf (1.06 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b13193" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of sodium-ion battery technol.  Future outlook of sodium-ion batteries is discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo_Iht8id70u7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgIteIe6d_KqA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFSiu7bL&amp;md5=d4497339c61083b2ec2c0568261e4909</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1007/s40843-015-0084-8"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Massé, R. C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Uchaker, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, G.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Beyond Li-Ion: Electrode Materials for Sodium-and Magnesium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Sci.                 10.1007/s40843-015-0084-8</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D58%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D715-766%26journal%3DSci.%2BChina%2BMater.%26author%3DR.%2BC.%2BMass%25C3%25A9%26author%3DE.%2BUchaker%26author%3DG.%2BCao%26title%3DBeyond%2BLi-Ion%253A%2BElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-and%2BMagnesium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs40843-015-0084-8&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b13193&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs40843-015-0084-8&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Beyond Li-ion: electrode materials for sodium- and magnesium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Masse, Robert C.; Uchaker, Evan; Cao, Guozhong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science China Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">58</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">715-766</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCMCDB</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2095-8226</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Science China Press</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The need for economical and sustainable energy storage drives battery research today.  While Li-ion batteries are the most mature technol., scalable electrochem. energy storage applications benefit from redns. in cost and improved safety.  Sodium- and magnesium-ion batteries are two technologies that may prove to be viable alternatives.  Both metals are cheaper and more abundant than Li, and have better safety characteristics, while divalent magnesium has the added bonus of passing twice as much charge per atom.  On the other hand, both are still emerging fields of research with challenges to overcome.  For example, electrodes incorporating Na+ are often pulverized under the repeated strain of shuttling the relatively large ion, while insertion and transport of Mg2+ is often kinetically slow, which stems from larger electrostatic forces.  This review provides an overview of cathode and anode materials for sodium-ion batteries, and a comprehensive summary of research on cathodes for magnesium-ion batteries.  In addn., several common exptl. discrepancies in the literature are addressed, noting the addnl. constraints placed on magnesium electrochem.  Lastly, promising strategies for future study are highlighted.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqeu1nepe2jR7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgIteIe6d_KqA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhslCqtQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=54ef894bc82bd710e90a1c23bb069d40</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1149/1.1379565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Stevens, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors compare the interactions of lithium and sodium with a range of carbon materials in electrochem. cells.  Through wide angle in situ x-ray scattering studies, both lithium and sodium can be inserted into the interlayer space in disordered carbon materials.  This insertion process is accompanied by an increase in the interlayer spacing in these materials.  Small-angle in situ scattering studies are presented to clearly show the insertion of lithium and sodium into nanopores within disordered hard carbons.  Also very little, if any, sodium can be inserted into graphitic materials in contrast to the large capacity seen for lithium insertion.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOuo4QAURxurVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgWsHERk5igiA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3MXlvFKntr0%253D&amp;md5=15fc03ac3de24271573ed94315375242</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1007/BF02645381"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Songster, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pelton, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The use is demonstrated of FeSe2 as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Few-Layered SnS2 on Few-Layered Reduced Graphene Oxide as Na-Ion Battery Anode with Ultralong Cycle Life and Superior Rate Capability</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yandong; Zhu, Peiyi; Huang, Liliang; Xie, Jian; Zhang, Shichao; Cao, Gaoshao; Zhao, Xinbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered as promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries due to the natural abundance of sodium resources.  Searching for high-performance anode materials currently becomes a hot topic and also a great challenge for developing Na-ion batteries.  In this work, a novel hybrid anode is synthesized consisting of ultrafine, few-layered SnS2 anchored on few-layered reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by a facile solvothermal route.  The SnS2/rGO hybrid exhibits a high capacity, ultralong cycle life, and superior rate capability.  The hybrid can deliver a high charge capacity of 649 mA-h/g at 100 mA/g.  At 800 mA/g (1.8C), it can yield an initial charge capacity of 469 mA-h/g, which can be maintained at 89% and 61%, resp., after 400 and 1000 cycles.  The hybrid can also sustain a c.d. up to 12.8 A/g (≈28C) where the charge process can be completed in only 1.3 min while still delivering a charge capacity of 337 mA-h/g.  The fast and stable Na-storage ability of SnS2/rGO makes it a promising anode for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq2sS3KKxaUkbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgWsHERk5igiA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvF2ms7jI&amp;md5=173a3c027a58922c59d5633c7a05d580</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2012.05.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ai, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, H.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report colloidal synthesis of Sb nanocrystals with mean size tunable in the 10-20 nm range and with narrow size distributions of 7-11%.  In comparison to microcryst. Sb, 10 and 20 nm Sb nanocrystals exhibit enhanced rate capability and higher cycling stability as anode materials in rechargeable Li-ion and Na-ion batteries.  All three particle sizes of Sb have high and similar Li-ion and Na-ion charge storage capacities of 580-640 mA-h/g at moderate charging/discharging current densities of 0.5-1C (1C rate is 660 mA/g).  At all C-rates (0.5-20C, e.g. current densities of 0.33-13.2 A/g), capacities of 20 nm Sb particles are systematically better than for both 10 nm and bulk Sb.  At 20C rate, retention of charge storage capacities by 10 and 20 nm Sb nanocrystals can reach 78-85% of the low-rate value, indicating that rate capability of Sb nanostructures can be comparable to the best Li-ion intercalation anodes and is so far unprecedented for Na-ion storage.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp5xNSHYkxGf7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0linp1EIhLDH2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhs1Wmsbw%253D&amp;md5=6c9a0c090f52077a9b8eda7847cbfe2f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.5b03373"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tan, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kalisvaart, P.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Janish, M.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Coupling In Situ TEM and Ex Situ Analysis to Understand Heterogeneous Sodiation of Antimony</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zhi; Tan, Xuehai; Li, Peng; Kalisvaart, Peter; Janish, Matthew T.; Mook, William M.; Luber, Erik J.; Jungjohann, Katherine L.; Carter, C. Barry; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We employed an in situ electrochem. cell in the transmission electron microscope (TEM) together with ex situ time-of-flight, secondary-ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS) depth profiling, and FIB-helium ion scanning microscope (HIM) imaging to detail the structural and compositional changes assocd. with Na/Na+ charging/discharging of 50 and 100 nm thin films of Sb.  TOF-SIMS on a partially sodiated 100 nm Sb film gives a Na signal that progressively decreases toward the current collector, indicating that sodiation does not proceed uniformly.  This heterogeneity will lead to local volumetric expansion gradients that would in turn serve as a major source of intrinsic stress in the microstructure.  In situ TEM shows time-dependent buckling and localized sepn. of the sodiated films from their TiN-Ge nanowire support, which is a mechanism of stress-relaxation.  Localized horizontal fracture does not occur directly at the interface, but rather at a short distance away within the bulk of the Sb.  HIM images of FIB cross sections taken from sodiated half-cells, elec. disconnected, and aged at room temp., demonstrate nonuniform film swelling and the onset of analogous through-bulk sepn.  TOF-SIMS highlights time-dependent segregation of Na within the structure, both to the film-current collector interface and to the film surface where a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) exists, agreeing with the electrochem. impedance results that show time-dependent increase of the films' charge transfer resistance.  We propose that Na segregation serves as a secondary source of stress relief, which occurs over somewhat longer time scales.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGob2HLjqRKWnrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0linp1EIhLDH2Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsFagtLvN&amp;md5=6c30a8cf04ee0cc7d664d3209bd6864d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1039/C5EE00878F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wen, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mi, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lei, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Large-scale Highly Ordered Sb Nanorod Arrays Anode with High Capacity and Rate Capability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Energy Environ.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Large-scale highly ordered Sb nanorod array anodes with high capacity and rate capability for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Liying; Xu, Yang; Wang, Chengliang; Wen, Liaoyong; Fang, Yaoguo; Mi, Yan; Zhou, Min; Zhao, Huaping; Lei, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are a potential substitute to Li-ion batteries for energy storage devices.  However, their poor electrochem. performance, esp. capacity and rate capability, is the major bottleneck to future development.  Here we propose a performance-oriented electrode structure, which is 1D nanostructure arrays with large-scale high ordering, good vertical alignment, and large interval spacing.  Benefiting from these structural merits, a great enhancement in electrochem. performance could be achieved.  Taking Sb as an example, we firstly report large-scale highly ordered Sb nanorod arrays with uniform large interval spacing (190 nm).  In return for this electrode design, high ion accessibility, fast electron transport, and strong electrode integrity are presented here.  Used as additive- and binder-free anodes for Na-ion batteries, Sb nanorod arrays showed a high capacity of 620 mA h g-1 at the 100th cycle with a retention of 84% up to 250 cycles at 0.2 A g-1, and a superior rate capability for delivering reversible capacities of 579.7 and 557.7 mA h g-1 at 10 and 20 A g-1, resp.  A full cell coupled by a P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 cathode and a Sb nanorod array anode was also constructed, which showed good cycle performance up to 250 cycles, high rate capability up to 20 A g-1, and large energy d. up to 130 Wh kg-1.
